KIDS, PLS *DO NOT EAT TOYS AND ANYTHING ELSE THAT IS NOT EATABLE* CUZ YOU CAN DIE IN A RANDOM DAY AND IN A RANDOM TIME
If you swallow a battery, especially a button battery, saliva can trigger an electrical current that causes a serious chemical burn to the esophagus or trachea, potentially leading to severe internal injury or death within hours. The symptoms can include drooling, coughing, chest pain, vomiting, or difficulty swallowing. It is a medical emergency that requires immediate attention at an emergency department, especially for children.  Immediate consequences Chemical burns: Saliva can activate the battery, causing an electrical current that burns through the esophagus or trachea in as little as two hours.  Tissue damage: This burn can cause severe tissue damage, potentially creating a hole through the airway, damaging vocal cords, or leading to life-threatening bleeding.  Obstruction: A battery lodged in the throat can cause choking or airway compromise.  Symptoms to watch for Drooling Coughing Wheezing Vomiting, possibly with blood Chest discomfort or pain Refusal to eat or drink Difficulty swallowing A feeling of a lump in the throat Abdominal pain  What to do Go to the emergency room immediately: A swallowed battery is a medical emergency. Do not delay: The time between ingestion and removal is critical to preventing irreversible damage, particularly for children.  If the battery is stuck in the nose or ear: Also seek immediate medical attention, as it is considered an emergency, according to Nemours KidsHealth.

If a letter block is swallowed, it usually passes through the digestive system without causing any harm. Most swallowed objects, including letter blocks, are harmless and will be passed in stool within a few days.  Here's a more detailed explanation: Swallowed objects typically pass through: Swallowed objects, including letter blocks, usually make their way to the stomach and then through the intestines, eventually being passed out in stool.  Most objects are harmless: In most cases, swallowing an object doesn't cause any problems. The object will simply pass through the digestive system on its own.  When to be concerned: While most swallowed objects are harmless, it's important to be aware that sharp, pointed, or caustic objects like batteries can cause serious problems.  When to seek medical advice: If you're concerned about a swallowed object, it's always a good idea to consult a healthcare professional, especially if the child is having trouble breathing, swallowing, or speaking, or if there are any other concerning symptoms.
Swallowing a Hot Wheels car is generally harmless. The small size of the toy makes it likely to pass through the digestive system. If swallowed, a Hot Wheels car is unlikely to pose a serious danger.  Here's why: Size: Hot Wheels cars are small and typically made of die-cast metal, which is relatively lightweight and can be easily passed through the digestive tract. Digestive System: The digestive system is designed to process food and waste, and small, non-toxic objects like a Hot Wheels car can usually pass through without causing issues. No Intestinal Blockage: While it's possible for very large or irregularly shaped objects to cause a blockage, a Hot Wheels car is unlikely to do so.  If you're concerned: Monitor for Symptoms: Keep an eye on the person who swallowed the car for any signs of discomfort, like abdominal pain or difficulty swallowing. Seek Medical Advice: If you have any concerns or if you notice any symptoms, it's always best to consult with a doctor or medical professional. Focus on Prevention: To prevent future incidents, ensure that children are supervised while playing with toys, and consider storing Hot Wheels and other small toys out of reach.
Eating an old Kinder Egg, while generally not harmful, might lead to a less than desirable taste or texture, or even some foodborne illnesses if the chocolate has been exposed to high temperatures or stored improperly. It's unlikely to be dangerous, but it's always a good idea to check the "best by" date and look for signs of spoilage like mold or unusual smells.  Potential issues with eating an old Kinder Egg: Taste: The chocolate might develop a "chocolate bloom," which is a white coating that can change the flavor and texture without causing harm, but can still be unpleasant.  Texture: The chocolate could become chalky or grainy if exposed to temperature fluctuations or stored incorrectly.  Spoilage: If the chocolate has been stored improperly or exposed to high temperatures, it could develop mold or become contaminated with foodborne illnesses.  "Best by" Date: Kinder products, like most food items, have a shelf life. While they might still be safe to eat after the date, they are not guaranteed to have the same quality.  Allergies: Some Kinder products can contain nuts or other allergens. If someone is allergic to an ingredient, it's important to be aware of the potential risks, regardless of the age of the egg.  Recommendation: Check the "best by" date to ensure the product is within its recommended shelf life.  Look for signs of spoilage, like mold or unusual smells.  Store the Kinder Egg in a cool, dry place to help preserve its quality.  If you're unsure about the age or quality of the Kinder Egg, it's always best to err on the side of caution and discard it.
If a person swallows a Lego, the plastic will likely pass through the digestive system and out with a bowel movement within a day or two. Most small, non-sharp objects ingested by humans and children pass through without causing any problems.  Here's a more detailed explanation: Passage through the digestive tract: Small, non-sharp objects like Lego pieces usually pass through the esophagus, stomach, and intestines without any issues.  Normal bowel movement: The Lego piece will likely be passed out with the stool.  Timeframe: It usually takes 1-3 days for a small plastic object like a Lego to clear the system.  No need for intervention: In most cases, there's no need for medical intervention.  When to seek medical advice: If the person has any difficulty breathing, pain, or other concerning symptoms, they should seek immediate medical attention.  Exceptions: In rare cases, larger objects or objects with sharp edges might require removal by a doctor.  In summary: Swallowing a Lego is usually not a cause for concern, as the Lego piece will likely pass through the digestive system and out with a bowel movement. However, it's always a good idea to monitor for any signs of distress and to seek medical advice if needed.
Swallowing a battery, especially a small button battery, can be extremely dangerous and potentially life-threatening. If the battery gets stuck in the throat or esophagus, it can cause a chemical reaction that leads to severe burns and tissue damage. Prompt medical attention is crucial in such cases.  Here's a more detailed explanation: Dangerous Chemical Reaction: When saliva comes into contact with a battery, it can trigger a chemical reaction that generates electricity and heat. This can cause burns to the tissues of the throat, esophagus, and even the stomach.  Risk of Getting Stuck: Button batteries are small and can easily get lodged in the esophagus, especially if the person swallows them without swallowing properly.  Potential Complications: Severe burns can lead to tissue damage, perforation of the esophagus, airway damage, and even death.  Symptoms: Symptoms of button battery ingestion can include coughing, drooling, trouble swallowing, chest pain, abdominal pain, and vomiting.  Medical Attention: If you suspect someone has swallowed a battery, seek immediate medical help.
